		<username>unbend_5440</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Joe Johnston knows how to make a movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I did have a few problems with Hidalgo, but the redeemable qualities more than makes up for Johnston's mistakes. What I have to start by saying is that this is a very classic action movie. I've noticed that throughout Joe Johnston's directing career, he's done an incredible job of paying tribute to genre's of the past. With Honey I Shrunk The Kids it was old disney kids fantasy movies, with October Sky it was Capra, with JP3 it was classic monster movies. What makes Hidalgo so great is that Johnston made the movie feel like a classic western adventure movie. He understands the genre, and doesn't get too deep into updating it to modern day standards. And Viggo Mortensen understands the genre as well. He doesn't worry about making himself come off as really slick and cool. He's just an average guy that's very heroic.Hidalgo isn't without flaw, and it's by no means Johnston's best work. It's at least 15 minutes too long, and there were some poor transitions about half way through. At one point a side story develops that takes us out of the race, and to me it felt like a second movie was going on for a while. The transition into this could have been done a little smoother. I also think this movie could have benefitted from more profiling of the racers. Only 2 other racers besides Frank Hopkins get any real screentime. At the beginning of both legs of the race, you see all the racers at once, but the race itself could have been a lot more exciting if we saw more than just the 3 main racers. You can't really get the maximum amount of tension when we're not even shown if there are more than 3 guys competing. But these are minor flaws.I liked that the action sequences relied more on clever staging than just fast cuts and lots of gunshots and fighting. What was so enjoyable about the action was that Frank Hopkins would do spontaneous things that I didn't see coming. The action was original and clever. It didn't try and be too serious. There was humor in almost every action scene from start to finish. And while I was having a great time through the whole movie, when it got to the ambush sequence with the buried trap and the leopard attack, this movie had officially won me over. That sequence was incredibly exciting. And the finale of the race worked really well. I think that's because it wasn't predictable. In most movies you'd just assume that the hero would come from behind and win at the last second, but I wasn't so sure what was going to happen. Maybe that's because Johnston never makes this movie about a guy that HAS to win. Hopkins and Hidalgo don't even seem too concerned with winning, just finishing. On a final note, I just want to comment that Omar Sharif really does steal the movie. I only wish he had been used more in the final act. 8 out of 10</fullReview>
	</review>

I also liked the horse, of how brave it was, all the dangerous things it did, how it listened to its master, and how it protected and aided him when needs be. There was mainly two problems with this film. One was that it seemed to go on forever. I wondered when it was time to leave, for it was in the evening. I was getting hungry. The other part was the ending. i won't give it away as much as I can, but he does not get the stunningly, attractive women. But, it was not an everyday adventure film, but its honestly worth seeing.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-03-08</date>
		<username>unbend_5440</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Joe Johnston knows how to make a movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I did have a few problems with Hidalgo, but the redeemable qualities more than makes up for Johnston's mistakes. What I have to start by saying is that this is a very classic action movie. I've noticed that throughout Joe Johnston's directing career, he's done an incredible job of paying tribute to genre's of the past. With Honey I Shrunk The Kids it was old disney kids fantasy movies, with October Sky it was Capra, with JP3 it was classic monster movies. What makes Hidalgo so great is that Johnston made the movie feel like a classic western adventure movie. He understands the genre, and doesn't get too deep into updating it to modern day standards. And Viggo Mortensen understands the genre as well. He doesn't worry about making himself come off as really slick and cool. He's just an average guy that's very heroic.Hidalgo isn't without flaw, and it's by no means Johnston's best work. It's at least 15 minutes too long, and there were some poor transitions about half way through. At one point a side story develops that takes us out of the race, and to me it felt like a second movie was going on for a while. The transition into this could have been done a little smoother. I also think this movie could have benefitted from more profiling of the racers. Only 2 other racers besides Frank Hopkins get any real screentime. At the beginning of both legs of the race, you see all the racers at once, but the race itself could have been a lot more exciting if we saw more than just the 3 main racers. You can't really get the maximum amount of tension when we're not even shown if there are more than 3 guys competing. But these are minor flaws.I liked that the action sequences relied more on clever staging than just fast cuts and lots of gunshots and fighting. What was so enjoyable about the action was that Frank Hopkins would do spontaneous things that I didn't see coming. The action was original and clever. It didn't try and be too serious. There was humor in almost every action scene from start to finish. And while I was having a great time through the whole movie, when it got to the ambush sequence with the buried trap and the leopard attack, this movie had officially won me over. That sequence was incredibly exciting. And the finale of the race worked really well. I think that's because it wasn't predictable. In most movies you'd just assume that the hero would come from behind and win at the last second, but I wasn't so sure what was going to happen. Maybe that's because Johnston never makes this movie about a guy that HAS to win. Hopkins and Hidalgo don't even seem too concerned with winning, just finishing. On a final note, I just want to comment that Omar Sharif really does steal the movie. I only wish he had been used more in the final act. 8 out of 10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-03-07</date>
		<username>livewire-6</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Mustang sally, or Been through the desert on a horse ...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"You can lead a horse to water, but you can't make him drink." That's how I felt going into "Hidalgo". The trailer, or preview, turned me right off. That shot of an approaching sandstorm reminded me too much of the silly special effects in "The Mummy". My roommate had to bribe me to see "Hidalgo". He paid my admission, and I said I'd pay him back if I liked the movie.Well, he won the bet, and so does Frank Hopkins (Viggo Mortensen) in the film. He races his painted horse, a wild mustang, against the finest purebred Arabian steeds in the world. Damned if he doesn't reach the finish line first after crossing a stretch of desert known as the "ocean of fire".But you knew that, didn't you? No spoiler there. To quote another saying, "Getting there is half the fun" -- and "Hidalgo" takes us for quite a ride.The cinematography is breathtaking, except for the obligatory slow motion as Hidalgo enters the final stretch. There were also several annoying shots of the horse gazing straight at the camera. I kept expecting Hidalgo to say, "This is the screwiest picture I've ever been in!" (Shades of the camel in "Road to Morocco".) Or else they were setting Viggo Mortensen up as Wilbur Post to Hidalgo's Mr. Ed.The costumes are splendid, especially the 19th-century hats, veils and bustles worn by actress Louise Lombard.Even the acting is pretty good. (Early on, however, Viggo Mortensen seems to be channeling John Wayne, right down to the trademark swagger, drawl and loose wrist.)The story may be based on a true-life adventure, but the plot is far-fetched at times. (I felt cheated when the film invoked a "deus ex machina", a divine intervention, to save Hopkins and his horse at a crucial juncture.) As for the score, don't get me started. It's a retread of the music from every Hollywood western since Tom Mix.Every so often, a movie comes along that causes me to ask myself, "Why was this film made and released at this particular time?" "Hidalgo" is such a film. The story took place a century ago, but has a certain resonance in our time -- one that left a bad taste in my mouth. The tale of an American who beats the Arabs at their own game was quite simply too reminiscent of the war in Iraq. Indeed, Iraq is mentioned a couple of times during the film. To top it all off, one of the two leading female characters, a sheik's daughter, is named Jazira. This echoes the name of the Arab news network, Al-Jazeera.(By contrast, last year's "The Last Samurai" tells a different story. It is set in the same time period, with a similar hero, who adapts to a foreign culture and learns from it, instead of the other way around.)To be fair, the villains in "Hidalgo" are of all races and nationalities -- from the American cavalrymen who massacre the Sioux at Wounded Knee, to the dastardly Aziz and Katib (brother and nephew to the sheik), to the ice-cold Englishwoman, Lady Davenport.The heroes also span the spectrum -- from the urbane sheik (played by the incomparable Omar Sharif), to his intrepid and freedom-loving daughter Jazira, to the wise Sioux chief.Movies have the potential to be more than just entertainment. They can create evocative images and convey meaningful messages with the power to shape our culture, the way we think and feel. In the wrong hands, "Hidalgo" could have dealt in negative stereotypes. It is to the director's credit that he does not go down that slippery slope. Rather, he shows how vastly different cultures can share each other's wisdom and join their hands in friendship.</fullReview>
	</review>
	<review>
		<date>2004-03-07</date>
		<username>Amelia (niemi86)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Trite but with interesting points</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>***Spoilers Herein***While the plot of Hidalgo was predictable and the ending was feel-good and it's the kind of movie you smile at and then watch if it's on TV again in a couple months, it was well made and brought up several interesting points. (I call it a movie because I'm not sure that it ought to be called a film.) As this movie is apparently not based on the claimed true story, the way things turn out to give a couple of nice points.First, the British Davenports were well-cultured people and accepting of Arab culture. Mrs. Davenport said something about living with them since she had been thirteen, but she still saw herself as slightly above them, as she made some comment about tribal war being the national sport. However, she did have great respect for their entire horse culture, a respect that was not shared either by her or her husband for American Indians. If I'm not mistaken, they both talked about the "Red Indians," showing how that culture was somewhat ignored - elbowed aside and better forgotten.Identity - who you are, who your ancestors were - played a big role in this movie. Frank Hopkins and Hidalgo were both 'mixed breeds,' as opposed to the purebreds (both horses and humans) they raced against. The three competitors racing for the finish line represented Britain, the Middle East, and America. The first two were all about breeding and ancestry and looked down on the "half-bred" American and his horse. This ancestry, the bloodline, became more important than the actual horse or person you were. Thus, the race becomes more a competition of your breeding than your desire to win. Hidalgo, representing the American melting-pot, is the winner of the race, showing that willpower can outrun even the best bred of horses. Hopkins is the ideal rugged American individual and didn't even accept his Indian heritage - rather, he accepted it but did not take pride or participate in it until the race.I had a bunch of other little points that I was going to mention here, but in the course of writing this I forgot them all, so after you've seen this movie, you can think about these or not think about these and maybe I'll think of more and post another comment later.</fullReview>
	</review>

Omar Sharif was excellent, even down to the gap in his front teeth. The photography was breathtaking and the ending made you feel good about these wonderful wild mustangs. They are becoming a dying breed and it is a shame. After the movie, we stopped off at the store to buy some chicken for a late dinner and highly recommended it to a few people. Take your family and enjoy a great adventure and film. We enjoyed how smart they showed Hidalgo.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>I Agree with Ebert</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I really enjoyed "Hidalgo". It was an old-fashioned western with excitement, adventure, beautiful scenery and two main characters you cared about. I thought the critics who disliked this film because Viggo Mortensen "mumbles" (which I don't think he does); Damascus is not on a body of water; the plot twists are unbelievable; miss the point. This is a fun movie. It's a tall tale. Viggo's Frank T. Hopkins is likeable and the horse really is a good "actor". I totally get the "mixed breed" issue. And what's wrong with being traumatized by an unjust and gut-wrenching event? Since Tom Cruise suffered in "Last Samarai" nobody else can explore the concept? Because "Seabiscuit" was released, we can't have anymore movies about horses or racing? Get a grip -- Hollywood has made these films time and again. If people would just lighten up, stop trying to be clever and rate a movie for what it's trying to do not what they think it should do, reviews would be a lot more helpful.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-03-07</date>
		<username>Gayle</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>One of the best movies I've ever seen</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This movie was fabulous from beginning to end. Viggo Mortensen alone makes the movie worth seeing, but stellar performances by the likes of Malcolm McDowell and Omar Sharif just add to the pleasure of the film. The relationship between Hopkins and the Arab Princess is sweet. In the tradition of his other films, Mortensen's development of the character lends a sense of authenticity to the entire thing. I read in an interview that he went and stayed with the Indian Tribe that speaks the language he had to learn for the movie in order to make his accent and pronunciations sound authentic. This is just one example of Mortensen's dedication as a method actor and true tendency to live his parts and not just play them. One of the best "actors" in the movie was the horse! What a sweet animal with a great personality! And Mortensen's obvious real life affection for the horse makes Frank Hopkins dedication to Hidalgo ring true and feel real.Don't miss this film! The cinematography is spectacular. The sound track is great. The acting is wonderful. All in all, it's a beauty!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Beautiful, entertaining, straightforward adventure</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I love this movie!!! It is beautifully photographed, to be sure, but the overall story is what makes it for me. It's true that the movie is VERY LOOSELY based on Frank Hopkins life and it has to be viewed as more of a tall tale than history, but many of the legends of the old West are embellishments of reality.The pace of the movie is just right to me and it never seemed to be long or boring. I am a big fan of Viggo Mortensen and was very pleased to see this solid performance in a starring role. Considering that it is a Disney movie, I was pleased that the movie was pretty straightforward in approach and didn't get overly artificial. It is an adventure movie, after all, so there are a number of contrived "situations" for the hero to overcome. Viggo's performance is typically quiet and introspective, which is appropriate for the character. His Indian chant toward the end moved me to tears.I also liked that there wasn't any artificial romance in the movie. He has respect for the Sheik's daughter but I never felt the movie implying "romance" on his part or hers. They are clearly from different worlds and it's going to stay that way. And he has a healthy distrust of the conniving British woman. I found her to be the real villain in the movie.Of course it's formulaic like just about any movie about racing or sports competition. The end can be predicted before the opening credits, you just get to enjoy the journey with the hero or the team.I have seen the movie twice and plan to see it several more times. It's beautiful, entertaining and very satisfying.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-03-06</date>
		<username>schappe1</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Seabiscuit meets Lawrence of Arabia, et al</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Mild Spoilers)Frank Hopkins was a real cowboy hero and Hidalgo was a real horse. Together they really did go to Arabia and win a 3,000 miles race against the finest Arabian horses and riders. Frank actually won this race by 33 hours rather than by a nose, as in this Hollywoodization of his achievement. But that is forgivable: this is an adventure film with some dramatic backbone to it, not a precise history. The screenwriter has incorporated several subplots that seem like something out of Raiders of the Lost Ark, with Frank departing from the race to save a beautiful chieftain's son from the clutches of a rival to the Arab Prince who is putting the race on. There's also an aristocratic English femme fatal who will do anything- even to the point of seducing the hero- to have her horse win. There are also many small points of humor, such as Frank getting riled up whenever somebody says anything bad about his horse, the Prince being a fan of western dime novels, the Prince supposedly having the ability to tell the future, (which he never does throughout the film), and which would be lost if an infidel like Frank should touch him. It's all good fun, if heavily derivative. Omar Sharif, 40 years after Lawrence of Arabia, plays the Prince, once again marveling over a foreigner's ability to conquer the desert. Scenes involving quicksand and a triumphant ride along the beach after the race are obviously inspired by the former film. Viggo Mortenson, who plays Frank, rides away from an oncoming sandstorm that seems like something out of Lord of the Rings. We are constantly reminded that Hidalgo is an old horse and is injured, so he is an underdog like Seabiscuit. The wrap-around story about the fate of the Sioux is out of Dances with Wolves. Hey, they were all good films.I was a little uncomfortable with a story of an American hero showing up the Arabs, who are shown as villainous or slightly comical in light of current events. How would this play to a Middle Eastern audience? I don't know. But if you accept it as just another adventure film, it's pretty good.</fullReview>
	</review>

It is a straight forward story, told in the slant of bright light and dark shadows; of good and evil.The grit of sand on wind and blazing sun, it is the private journey of a man to become 'whole' and a celebration of the empathic kinship between man and animal. 		<date>2004-03-06</date>
		<username>Rosematuse</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Enjoyable for the entire family.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>My husband, mother, grand-daughter, and myself all went to see Hidalgo and thoroughly enjoyed it. If you plan to see this likable film you might not want to read on as some plot spoilers appear in the next paragraphs. It's the story of a man accepting who he is and the help he gets from his new human friends and from his loyal feisty mustang named Hidalgo. Mr. Mortensen does a fantastic job in his first 'starring' role as the emotionally damaged Frank Hopkins, a dispatch long distance rider for the government. Frank has to fight his way out of the depressed and alcoholic existence he fell into after delivering a dispatch that resulted in the massacre at Wounded Knee. Somehow he feels responsible for the slaughter which sends him into a tailspin. He winds up working for a Wild West show that gives him the money to live on but not the will. During one of the shows he is challenged by an Arab man to enter the Ocean of Fire endurance race and prove his claim as the best endurance racer in the world. Encouraged to take up the challenge to enter the mother of all long distance endurance races he is financed by his friends and goes on a journey that for him is life changing as he comes to grips with the pain of denying his Indian heritage throughout his adult life. Mr. Mortensen has an expressive face and uses it to his advantage in conveying his journey into a world that is alien to him and to his horse. His charisma that makes him likable even as a villain in some of his previous movies comes through in this role in spades. He equestrian talents are awesome and who needs special effects when you witness his thrilling ride in the sand storm scene that to me (having actually been in some sand storms) looks very real. The locations are beautiful and the editing is well done. The supporting cast is effective and round out the new world that Frank Hopkins and Hidalgo enter into. The special effects are really quite good and I think people today are too nit-picky about each and every effect and are just looking for mistakes instead of just sitting back and enjoying a good tale. I can tell you this; there is no nudity, no sex (some innuendos), some rip roaring fight scenes with non-gory violence, and only a mild curse word or two. I guess for some people that makes a movie boring but to us it was great and we would recommend it to anyone who wants to take the whole family and enjoy an evening at the movies.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>(winner55)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Wounded Knee and American imperialism</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film has come under attack for being a fiction masquerading as a true story. On rewview of these criticisms, their tone suggests to me that what these critics find unpleasant is not the story of a horse race through the Sahara; although they don't say so, what they fear is the very well documented facts concerning the Massacre at Wounded Knee - presented in an honest and even-handed manner in this film - and the equally well-established desire by certain politicians in America to portray Islam as a "primitive" culture requiring immediate corrective invasion on the part of American soldiers and businesses; when, in fact, as the film shows, Moslems are a civilized people struggling to develop a civilized solution to pretty standard human failings in an environment that presents human survival itself with challenges every day.I do not care whether the autobiography of the leading character (upon which the film is based) is true, or if it were a false self-engrandisement. The larger truths presented by the film, concerning American attitudes towards Native Americans and towards Moslems, needs repeated demonstration in artistic media, regardless of source material.This film is a pretty good popularization of the real issues. Now we need in-depth exposure of the events at wounded knee, and of the recent American efforts to colonize the Mid-East.Just by the way, this film is a lot of fun; don't get the impression that it is didactic in any way, it is not. It is truly "epic" in a way few such recent efforts have succeeded at. The hero is heroic, a very noble equine, and the hero's rider is heroic too; they both act very well, in fact the whole cast turns in wonderful performances, professionally presented by an excellent production.Don't let the cranks, right or left republican, spoil this film in advance - see it and enjoy.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-10-17</date>
		<username>m0rg16</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Slow paced adventure flick</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I didn't have very high expectations on this film, but how hard could it be to make an entertaining adventure flick? Apparently, Joe Johnston, director of Jurassic Park 3, found it to be pretty hard.The film starts out with Viggo Mortensen's character Frank Hopkins with witnessing a slaughter of an Indian tribe, which is very badly edited. I felt nothing for the Indians cause it came so sudden and the tone was anti-dramatic. Not a very good start. It is later revealed he is part Indian, and a very good horse rider. He and his horse Hidalgo has won several endurance races throughout their years. The word of Hidalgo and Frank has spread across the Atlantic, to the Sheikh of Sheikhs (Omar Sharif).The Sheikh offers Frank to participate in the annual "Ocean of Fire" race, which spans 3000 miles across the Arabian desert. Frank agrees, and travels across the Atlantic to Arabia. Along the way, we find the obvious reason why this film is so ludicrous: Malcolm McDowell in a cameo! Augh! As he arrives to Arabia, he meets the Sheikh and a bunch of other characters who find the entrance of an "infidel" to be "sacrilege". Everyone tells him he will be among the first to perish in the Arabian desert. Soon after, the race starts and Frank and Hidalgo take off.In the scenes with Frank in the Arabian camp, a lot of culture clash scenes occur. Their purpose is to show that we, the US, are more civilized than the Arabians. Well, there's one scene that absolutely stuck to my mind. When Frank first arrives to the Arabian camp, he rides past a few poorly dressed people being shoved away. One of his guides says something along the lines of: "Have you ever seen a slave market before?" to which Frank responds with a kind of grimace, reminding the audiences that "No, we are the USA, home of the free.". But if this film takes place in 1890, that was only 27 years after Abraham Lincoln abolished slavery. Nice tough, Joe Johnston, better check on your history next time.The action scenes (This is an adventure film after all) are few, poor and far apart. The most enjoyable one has to be when they visit a thief market and a fight breaks out, where a kick ass Nubian rips out his sable and with some really nice swordplay defeats a handful of brigands. As with every other sympathetic character (apart from Frank, the Sheikh and his daughter) he gets shot in the stomach. I'm not kidding, almost every character in this film suffers the very same fate. It's a shame too. I wish the film would have focused on his character, instead of Viggos (not that Viggo is a bad actor, but his character is 100% 1-dimensional). Other action scenes includes: A short flight from a sandstorm, a short climactic fight with the main bad guy, a short (and poorly done) scene where the bandits attack the Arabian tent. Some really poor swordplay choreography is shown here. (I also want to add that the film is incredibly long and somewhat slow paced. Tends to get boring at times.)Now, now, not everything about this film is bad. I mean, one of the main characters is Omar Sharif. That can't be bad! He plays his usual character, sympathetic Arab who wants well (why change a winning concept?). The special effects are quite nice. I really liked the short sequence where two cat creatures attack Viggo and his friend. Some really nice animation there! The music is perhaps not James Newton Howards best, but it's pretty good. Nothing that sticks to your mind, however. Also, the ending is incredibly beautiful and brought a tear to my eye. Very touching indeed!All in all, not a very good film. But if you're a fan of Viggo, horse movies and adventure flicks, then this film is perhaps something for you. But beware, it's very long and not that action packed.5/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I just wanted to let everyone know that this is NOT based on a true story as is claimed. There are no records of the main character or the horse in history. 		<shortDescription>Could have been a remarkable Horse Race Story-They screwed it up.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Caution - Spoilers They took what could have been a remarkable action - adventure story. A 3,000 mile horse race through the Arabian desert; and turned it into a silly Hollywood fantasy. - However it contains all of the elements needed to create a popular movie in today's American culture - It has the 'Noble Savage' motif - the wise old American Indians are shown as wise, honorable, long suffering and generally pacifist - their religion is superior to the 'white man's' as well; as when the hero chants in his own Sioux language for help - and it produces a turn-a-round. - A three thousand mile race of ninety nine Arabians versus one American seems like it would produce dozens of subplots of its own. But the producers choose to have the evil relative trying to take over the Top Sheiks kingdom, the abduction of his daughter and the American having to take a detour to save the Sheik's daughter in the middle of this huge race. How can the American's horse out run the bandit's horses time after time when the bandit's horses are fresh and the Hero's horse has been doing nothing but desert running for days upon days? The hero loses his gun in one scene and suddenly he has it two scenes later. The Sheik is going to castrate the hero for spending 5 minutes alone with his daughter - but later in the movie nothing is said when they spend the night together out in the desert alone ? They trade a counterfeit horse for the daughter - why did they have to take a white horse and paint it black ? Couldn't they find a black horse with the same features - come - on ! I was also surprised when the Sheik was ready to slay a traitor and the American Hero intervened to save him so that the American could torture him by placing his lasso around his neck, tying it to his horse and threatening to pull his head off. I thought torture and degradation were bad and interrogation with no threats and no physical harm - good. At least the movie ends with the freeing of hundreds of wild horses that the evil American army had captured and were going to senselessly slaughter. Letting all those horses go free - - it was just - - up lifting. It made me feel good.But I still give this movie a '3'.</fullReview>

Ooohhh, and lets put in bear traps and injure the horse...but he cant be lame because hes gotta win..."Thats the major point that bugged me, the great gaping wound that Hidalgo suffers yet he remains totally sound. Anyone with horses knows that the smallest puncture wound can make a horse lame let alone a 12 inch stab wound across a shoulder!But never mind, there are many more discrepancies you may find. but forget them and enjoy this film for what it it. A heart warming story with great central performances. Viggo is fantastic, as is Omar Sharrif, and enjoy the chemistry Viggo has with the horses... My vote, 8 out of 10</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Viggo Mortensen plays Frank Hopkins, the ex-Pony Express rider who made a name for himself in Buffalo Bill's Wild West Show late in the nineteenth century, with his horse, (the titular animal in question). Like "Seabiscuit," don't look for much more than the Hollywood version of actual history, although Hopkins does witness the bloody injustices of the Wounded Knee massacre in the film's first dramatic scenes. Unlike the biscuit, though, the story kicks into high gear adventure when Hopkins and his horse are invited to a race across the desert (through Iraq, no less), at the behest of a Western obsessed Sheik (Omar Sharif, who, forty years on from "Lawrence of Arabia," gets to swing quite a mean sword! Go, Omar!). Along the way, there are lovely women: Zuleikha Robinson (whom we two or three fans of the short lived X-Files spinoff, "The Lone Gunmen," will remember as Yves Adele Harlow) and Louise Lombard, but it's really all about a man and his horse. Another film to adequately tide you over until the next Indiana Jones flick. In fact, maybe Spielberg would consider recasting with a certain former Aragorn.? Hey, he could knock another twenty years out of that franchise, don't you think?</fullReview>

		<fullReview>So there I am, on my way to Leicester Square to see "Hidalgo," when I suddenly realise that the movie's written by John Fusco. The John Fusco who wrote "Young Guns." The John Fusco who wrote "Young Guns II." The John Fusco who wrote "Spirit: Stallion of the Cimarron." None of which bodes well for this latest horsey extravaganza, but by this time it's too late to turn back.And "Hidalgo" lives down to the writer's past track record; the titular horse and the rest of his four-legged brethren outact both Viggo Mortensen (as real-life endurance rider Frank Hopkins) and most of the rest of the cast, with the exception of Omar Sharif - as the sheikh involved with the deadly overland race that our hero is persuaded to enter with his beloved Mustang, he provides the movie with some genuine strength and charisma. (A far too low-key Mortensen, on the other hand, seems to have lost any presence when he went to the barber's after finishing up as Aragorn. Viggo Mortensen; the Samson of the 21st century.)Some interesting moments are all but lost throughout the two-hours-plus, and it's only occasionally rousing (mainly towards the end). But it's impossible to really see the point of the race, even allowing for the characters's regularly pointing out that Hopkins isn't part of their world; you just can't connect to the goings-on, despite writer Fusco and director Joe Johnston taking pains to underline what a sensitive, animal-friendly guy our hero is.A big expanse of not much, rather like the desert in which most of the movie takes place, I have to admit I mainly went to see this to check out the James Newton Howard score - which, not for the first time, is far better than the movie it supports.</fullReview>

		<username>FairiShadows</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Critics don't know everything ~Possible SPOILERS~</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Whatever you were expecting when you walked into the movie theatre you might as well throw it out with the styrofoam popcorn. If you've read the numerous lackluster reviews of Hidalgo and thought it wasn't worth it to see this film, I have one word for you: GO!WHY? You ask... 'Cuz they were dead wrong for starters...Hidalgo is a throwback to how they used to make films. There's a story, a few subplots and some good 'ol fashion guns and punching someone's lights out. The movie was well paced. I wasn't sure if I could sit through 2 and half hours of horses, but the movie was over before I was even settled into my seat. There aren't 100 million dollars worth of flashy special effects nor huge explosions and pretty images floating around the screen. If you enjoy beautiful vistas, edge of your seat action and good classic movies then this season Hidalgo's your best bet.Viggo Mortensen proves that he doesn't just play a king in the movies, he IS the king. From the moment he appears on the screen till the credits roll he oozes the masculinity of a John Wayne and the charisma and humor (and let's not forget the eyes to match) Paul Newman in Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid. The man carries the film and in doing so proves that he can be a leading man with a sword or his horse. I'll warn you though, if you go into Hidalgo thinking you're going to get The Lord of the Rings then yes, you're going to be disappointed. It doesn't even come close. Don't let Barry Bonds fool you however, you can't hit a homerun every time at the plate. A double into center driving in two runs is just as good though! And this movie is the series clincher. That being said, Viggo Mortensen does bring one thing from his role as Aragorn. He still has the juice to make you believe in the character. When he's singing the song of his people out in the desert at a pivotal point in the film, your heart aches for him. You can care about this guy and you'll want to see him win. You'll even want to bring him home for dinner. Indeed, Viggo is the shining the star.It's not to say that the rest of the cast is horrible, quite the contrary. Omar Sharif was brilliant and lovable in his own right as the Sheikh. The rest of the cast did exactly what it was supposed to. They supported the film and filled out it's bones. There are no standout performances but not every film needs nor should have them. They gave steady performances and enhanced the overall feel of the story.As wonderful as it is, there are some cons to this movie. The story isn't wonderful, the writing only adequate and the development leaves a little to be desired. There are a few subplots that should be fleshier if they are important or left out entirely and some were essential I believe in order to enhance film. It's not all that imaginitive and yes, it is predictible. You have to remember that this is NOT a thriller, however. There are also some definite stereotypes that yes, in this day and age might be a little perhaps insensitive, but one must also remember that it was set in 1890 not 2004. The overuse of the word 'infidels' and the Arabic views of The American might make you cringe, but unfortunately then, and perhaps even now the words ring true of their feelings. And not just of Arabs. We all are guilty of arrogance. The truth does sting a bit, but you cannot deny it. There are times that Viggo mutters his lines in the cowboy fashion he was going for and that can be irritating not being sure of what he said but doesn't take much from the film.It is also extremely important to not get caught up in the 'based on a true story' tagline. If you go in with any knowledge of the supposed story you'll miss the movie and what's the point in wasting 8 bucks if you're not going to let the work stand on it own regardless of the stigma attatched. Remember, it's BASED on a true story not a RECREATION.All in all, my recommendation is to go see this film. It's a good Sunday afternoon movie with the family that reminds us that when we accept and believe in ourselves; the impossible can happen.</fullReview>

Its importance as a film is chiefly in giving Viggo Mortensen the experience of playing a cowboy -- experience that he would later put to good use in Appaloosa.Mortensen plays Frank Hopkins, a former Pony Express courier, turned Buffalo Bill's Wild West Show actor, turned long distance horse racer in the Ocean of Fire--a 3,000 mile survival race across the Arabian desert. 		<date>2005-07-28</date>
		<username>Jonathon Dabell (barnaby.rudge@hotmail.co.uk)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Rather far-fetched version of an alleged true story.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Hidalgo claims of itself that it is based on a true story, but in reality it merely takes the skeleton of a true story and fleshes it out with a great deal of romanticised, far-fetched narrative trimmings. The cinematography is the film's strong point, with some genuinely excellent lensing of the American West and Arabian locations (Morocco standing in for 19th Century Arabia, Iraq and Syria). In other departments, the film is a somewhat average-or-worse affair. The script is full of clichés; the acting is quite lazy; and the outcome is predictable from the very beginning.Long distance horse rider Frank T. Hopkins (Viggo Mortensen) is famed for winning countless long distance races aboard his trusted mustang Hidalgo. Frank, the son of a cavalry soldier and a Red Indian woman, is traumatised after witnessing the massacre of unarmed Sioux Indians at Wounded Knee, so he retreats into a downward spiral of alcoholism while touring the USA with a rather degrading and historically-distorted Wild West Show. A shot at redemption comes Frank's way when he is challenged to enter the "Ocean Of Fire" race in Arabia. Upset by claims that Hidalgo is the greatest long distance race horse in the world, Arab Sheikh Riyadh (Omar Sharif) has decided to invite Frank and his mustang to compete in the gruelling 3000 mile desert endurance test against Arabia's finest thoroughbred horses and riders. The race is so dangerous that half the entrants will not survive, becoming lost in the unforgiving desert, or swallowed up by quicksand, or swept into oblivion by sudden and devastating sandstorms. Not merely endangered by the race itself, Frank and Hidalgo also face the hatred of fellow riders (unhappy about the presence of this foreigner in their midst); treachery from Anne Davenport (Louise Lombard), an English lady with ulterior motives for seeing her own horse win the race; and the unwanted advances of Jazira (Zuleikha Robinson), the sheikh's tempestuous daughter who is desperate for Hidalgo to win to spare her from an undignified marriage to the race favourite, an Arab prince with four wives already.Hidalgo certainly looks as if it has been put together at considerable expense, with lots of large-scale set pieces, and difficult but authentic location work (indeed, the DVD version features a documentary entitled Sand &amp; Celluloid which shows how challenging it was for film-makers to shoot in the desert). But the problems, as mentioned, rather overwhelm the film's strengths. For example, in the film's final moments Hidalgo is seriously wounded by a spear and Frank is all set to shoot the horse to put it out of its pain. But when he realises how close they are to the end, Frank puts away his gun and rides Hidalgo at full gallop to the finish line (passing two perfectly healthy stallions in the process!!) It's a pitifully unrealistic sequence, not to mention an over-used Hollywood cliché - just how often have we seen the heroes on the brink of giving up reviving themselves in time for a rousing finale? Mortensen's performance is incredibly detached, almost as if he has been blackmailed into appearing in the film and cannot be bothered to hide his irritation. I enjoyed him in The Lord Of The Rings trilogy but here he is a blank and unenthusiastic participant. All in all, Hidalgo is a bit of a let-down. Not a total bomb, but definitely not as interesting or intriguing as it should've been. I'd stick with Bite The Bullet, which has a similar story but is a whole lot better.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>tedg (tedg@filmsfolded.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Engineered</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Spoilers herein.I've spent time in a large script engineering lab. That's where they confabulate something like this.Start with a timely notion: a noble American in Iraq, employing honest grit and will in the face of Islamic fate and goofy beliefs. But this must be a true American, so make him half Indian. That way he can also be of a `people of the hoof.'Then triangulate among three formulas: `Lawrence of Arabia' so you can be cinematic with sand (and even reanimate Sharif); `Seabiscuit' which takes the theme of the little mongrel horse up against `purebloods' (here transformed from the `liberal' politic of the original to `conservative'); and `Last Samurai' so we can borrow the drunk who carries the guilt of an Indian massacre to a dead life in a wild west show.Triangulation is unfortunately a standard in these recipes. Then season with a couple women, a couple villains (better if they overlap), some parental conflict, some effects and a few other standard mechanisms. Then cook and promote.I do willingly enter the theater expecting to be manipulated, and yes for profit. But there's something intolerably crass about this sort of mechanical button-pressing.There was some extra story about the hawk saving the day at the end: more not quite subliminal reference to Bush. But test audiences took it out. Good for them.Ted's Evaluation -- 1 of 3: You can find something better to do with this part of your life.</fullReview>

		<date>2016-02-03</date>
		<username>NateWatchesCoolMovies</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Rollicking adventure</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When I was younger, and first got into seeing movies seriously in theatres, Lord Of The Rings was a huge thing. Every actor in it was scrutinized and put on a pedestal of worship by myself and peers whose passion lay in film. Many of the actors in that trilogy were for us at a young age relatively unknown, prompting strict attention as to what their further ventures beyond Middle Earth would be. Viggo Mortensen, being one of the stars of the show in LOTR, was naturally under my burgeoning cinematic microscope and therefore I was eager to rush out and see whatever else he did during and post Aragon. That's what led me to Hidalgo, a rousing western adventure about aging cowboy Frank T. Hopkins, who takes his beloved wild mustang hidalgo for one last race, aptly named the Ocean Of Fire. It's a brutal slow through hundreds of miles of hellish desert situated in the Syrian gulf and on through Damascus, with every earthly obstacle imaginable chomping at his spurs. Hopkins is a shadow of his former racing glory and enters the contest as a last ditch effort to rekindle dimming glory, and at the request of a wealthy sheik (always nice to see Omar Sharif) whose interest has been piqued by Frank's infamy. And so he enters, not quite knowing what fresh hell he is in for, and the movie takes off into pure joyous escapism. Mortensen already proved with LOTR that he makes an iron solid hero, and here he dons the cloak of rugged underdog with equal fire and brimstone. The photography starts in magisterial Montana and moves to the scorched beauty of the Arabian desert as he and hidalgo hurl themselves into the challenge of both their lifetimes in attempt to win gold and glory. Along the way he encounters many obstacles including bandits, plagues, the mother of all sandstorms and the sheik's gorgeous daughter Jazira (Zuleikha Robinson, excellent). There's nice supporting work from Louise Lombard, C. Thomas Howell, J.K. Simmons and a sly cameo from Malcolm McDowell. It's old school adventure done right, with dazzling scenery and a pillar of a lead performance from Mortensen.</fullReview>

		<date>2013-03-28</date>
		<username>johnnyboyz</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>"Hidalgo means NOBLEMAN... Viggo Mortensen is HIDALGO!" Oh, hold on - the 'horse' is Hidalgo...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What on Earth did Viggo Mortensen do wrong that meant he had to spend two and a half hours in the scorching desert talking to horses and eating bugs? Whatever it was, it must have been pretty damned evil of him. We expect to see the likes of Viggo Mortensen in roles such as these: confident, rugged men atop steeds and pretty handy in conflict; a post-Aragorn role for someone who seeks adventure and isn't the man to panic in the face of danger. But come the end of the film, we're surprised at how well he's done NOT to look like his "Carlito's Way" character; a tired, withered man beaten up by life and the absurd challenges he decided to take on therein it. In a directorial sense, this meek 2004 action/romance hybrid is precisely the sort of episodic nonsense we've come to expect from director Joe Johnston, the man responsible for the likes of Jumanji and Jurassic Park III, but here running out of luck with an often excruciated, almost certainly overly long, race film about respectable Westerners heading off to the untamed Arab world to prove his superiority.Viggo Mortensen plays Frank Hopkins, a Sioux Native American with the most un Sioux-like of names. He is a long distance rider/racer in this, the 1890s; a man who's attained fame for such things out of his skills in being a courier during the wars between indigenous Native Americans and white, European invaders. With his horse, the titular Hidalgo, they form what is considered the greatest long distance riding partnership of all time. They're racing on the American frontier in the very first scene, Hopkins established as a man unafraid of gamesmanship if it means winning against an arrogant rival; a man who's able to saddle up in the face of adversity before charging on ahead and pulling off a sensational win versus someone who was assured of victory. With a strong tie to his horse, cool in conflict and a hard bodied characteristic to boot, Hopkins is your man.Burying the pain of a massacre bestowed upon his people some years ago, and away from being in his element on the race circuit, Hopkins bites his lip and works with his horse on a cheesy, politically incorrect stage show ? one that is tacky and phony on top of the fact it talks down Native American Indians. A release arrives in the form of a chubby, arrogant Arab who's over from the Middle East with his aides, one of whom looks suspiciously like that sinister looking chap decked out in black from Raiders of the Lost Ark, to lay down to Hopkins the challenge of competing in his annual endurance race having observed the title bestowed upon Hopkins and Hidalgo that they are "the world's greatest distance horse and rider". The race itself is across a few thousand miles in the searing desert heat, a grueling contest against some of the best long distance riders in the world; most of whom will be locals feeding off the home advantage.Accepting, Hopkins suddenly finds himself in the firing line of slurs; intimidation and that of being demeaned when amongst these established racers, people who find the presence of this American and his show pony about to undertake this endurance race quite amusing. Before anything along these racing lines actually begins, Johnston bogs us down with a sub-plot involving the race organiser's daughter, Jazira (Robinson), and the fact that he who wins the race will have her as his wife. This, of course, relegates the lone female presence is relegated to that of being a prize awaiting the champion - although, this IS the Arab world circa. the late nineteenth century, so should the film be pulled up on its gender politics, or indeed hailed as a realistic depiction of hardships in a specific place at a specific time. Since the film has zero faith in the race itself being a dramatic enough entity, one of the local Arabic riders partaking in the race wants Jazira himself anyway, champion or otherwise. This will, of course, induce the later required conflict. To an extent, you cannot blame the film for such an approach; previous pieces of its ilk, such as Rat Race or The Cannonball Run, have called upon comedy to see it through its racing segments so as to keep us occupied. Hidalgo likes to think it's based on true events, and heading down the comedic passage would be, you would think, detrimental to the legacy of they who it is that's being depicted.The race itself is a curiosity, a contest wherein the participants ferociously duke it out over first place during the very early stages of the competition before all catching up with each other anyway come the evening when they arrive at a checkpoint. As things roll on, we're left sitting there waiting for some sort of sabotage to rear up, some kind of cheating ? sure enough, there is. Then, there's a sandstorm; also, a rivalry with a British trainer rears up, but oddly enough they do not suffer the resentment from the Arab hosts that our lead does in spite of the fact they're pompous and Western and female. Even though there is fierce racing across great distances, the likes of this trainer magically appear at the next rest stop ready to give Hopkins more verbal grief ? but how did they get there so quickly? The earlier nonsense to do with Jazira and the suitors she desires; those who desire her and those of whom her father would like her to marry, rear up on cue and cause the film to just come across as a completely different entity. The horse is too anthropomorphic; the film is no fun and to keep yourself occupied, you have to wonder just what in the world it was that those behind this were thinking.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>jspwordieitaly</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Man and horse as mutual metaphors</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>However far-fetched this ripping yarn may be, it is undeniably clever, moving and enjoyable. Other reviewers for this have missed the key point, that the hero's horse is a brilliant metaphor of himself. Hidalgo is a tongue-in-cheek reference to a Spanish aristocratic naming convention, meaning 'The son of', while Mustang is most likely ultimately etymologically tied to the Latin word for 'mixed'. Both rider and horse are half-breeds, taking on the finest Arabian thoroughbreds. I watched this film with a class full of Saudi male students of English. Apart from the whiskey, there was nothing I felt to be too strong for Islamic conventions here. The students were charmed by the classical Arabic of Omar Sharif, and the respect shown by the hero for the Arab world.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>This film promises to be entertaining and captivating given its unusual subject of an American cowboy racing in the Arabian desert at the end of the 19th century. Apart from the controversy surrounding the idea of the film, i.e. whether it's really based on a true story, the film is poor in every respect. From the start it is clear that no respect for history, geography or cultural accuracy has been attempted - it is just assumed that no one watching will really know anything about that time... The film focuses so much on the calamities besieging the hero, that it delivers very little other than sequences of scenes. The hero is miscast; it is hard to think that Disney could not find an American actor to play Hopkins; Mortensen spends the film trying so hard to be American that he forgets to act the part he's asked to play. You never connect with the story, or the hero, you never sense any connection between him and his mustang. The clichés abound and there is no point rooting for him anyway you know what's going to happen. This is just another cookie-cutter 'adventure' film with lots of special effects (but some great desert shots), another missed opportunity to make a story interesting and relevant with so many potential themes here, from American Indians,the presence of the British in Arabia, the(anti-?) hero himself and his motives. 		<fullReview>Frank (Viggo Mortensen) is a half Native American rider in Buffalo Bill's traveling show some one hundred years ago. Frank was a witness to an Indian massacre at Wounded Knee, among other injustices to Native Americans, and he now drinks too much in an attempt to forget the pain. His wonderful mustang horse, Hidalgo, saves him time and again as they perform in the circus tents. One night, an Arab gentleman congratulates Frank and Hildalgo on their victories in several endurance races on the plains but insists that the duo can not truly brag until they have entered and won a legendary race in Arabia. With monetary support from colleagues, Frank and Hidalgo go overseas and enter the contest, which begins in the deserts of Iraq and ends in Damascus. Can these two American champions truly compete with some of the best horses and riders in the world? Then, too, a rich Arab gentleman (Omar Sharif) has promised his daughter in marriage to a man who already has four wives. However, if Frank and Hidalgo win the race, this marital agreement is null and void. With sandstorms, outlaws, and sink holes along the way, is it too much to hope that Frank and Hildalgo will be victorious? This is a great and very exciting adventure movie, done in grand style. If you wish to present your loved ones with a family film that is the essence of great movie making, here is the flick for you. Despite its somewhat lengthy running time, this motion picture will enthrall viewers, from eight to eighty, in a big, big way.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-08-05</date>
		<username>Poe-17</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Accurate Schmaccurate</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I usually don't add my two cents to movies with as many reviews as this film has generated and while I will drop those two cents in a moment I want to address a recurring irritation I've seen expressed over and over with films "based on a true story".These films, like this one, often get slammed for historical inaccuracies. Aren't all films fiction? No matter how historically accurate they attempt to be they are ultimately actors giving their "take" on actual persons and writers imagining how these historical characters thought and acted and reacted in little moments in history and directors re-creating history as they envision it.A historical argument regarding a film is one subject but I don't see how a fictionalization of a "true story" can be docked, regarding entertainment value, because it bent the truth to create a work of entertainment.I think, in a preface to Goldman's screenplay for "Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid" the author, or someone, made the statement; "not that it matters, but most of what follows is true". That tells me that those incidents and dates that are verifiable are in the film and the balance in invented from whole cloth which, no matter how historically accurate it is, it remains a work of fiction.Hidalgo is based on a true story. That doesn't mean it presents itself as a true story, or even tried to keep itself as close to the truth as possible. The "true story" thing is akin to the other disclaimer "inspired by real events".Documentaries are true stories and real events.I asked of Hidalgo, as I do all the films I see, that it touch me in some way or in multiple ways, so that when it is over, I am glad I spent my time watching it. Storytelling is built into our ancient ancestry, movies are the modern version of someone spinning yarns around a campfire.My two cents; Hidalgo delivered. It entertained. It made me feel good to have seen it. All the elements that moved all the other contributers to reviews of this movie, moved me as well.It will go into my DVD collection though I fully know its relationship to the truth is fragile and whispy. It was a good, solid film, with lots of rewards for movie-folks in lots of categories.My favorite element; the human/animal bond. I had a horse in my youth (my first method of transportation) and shared most of my years since with canines. The human/animal bond thing is very real and very strong and sometimes kind of spooky.This is a very good movie. Likely inaccurate which leads me to paraphrase a very great screenwriter "Not that it matters, but most of what follows is made up".</fullReview>
	</review>

Viggo Mortenson with a cowboy hat.When Frank Hopkins (Aragorn...I mean, Mortenson) and Hidalgo are out casted from their once glorious lifestyle, they decide to take the ride of a lifetime and race in the 'Ocean of Fire': a hellishly long race across the Middle-Eastern desert, from Arabia to Damascas. Frank enters the race but he faces a very big problem: un-pedigreed horses in classy races are heavily frowned upon and Frank ain't making many friends since his horse is a mustang. Despite this major setback, Frank gives the race a shot and rides his best to glory."Hidalgo" would've been a great movie if it stayed to the storyline. While one will expect misadventures for the characters on their journey, the movie has many subplots that keep one impatient. The most obvious one is this doozy: A royal's daughter is kidnapped by some wild riders who will agree to give her back unless they receive the horse Al-Mataal, the purest, wildest, most beautiful horse in the Middle-East. They also seek a special horse breeding journal (stay with me) that carefully explains how to breed the most perfect race horses. Tell me what the original plot was!I'll give "Hidalgo" 6/10. Certainly a disappointment but still worthy of a rental.</fullReview>

		<username>Gene Crokus</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Bring 'Em On</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Using the not-dissimilar American West and inner reaches of northern Africa for settings about a fine American horseman (and a cowboy to boot)is one of the joys of this Hollywood reach-film. In true fiction movie fashion a somewhat mythical figure (Frank T. Hopkins played by Viggo Mortensen) is challenged by Arabs jealous of his horse and Hopkins' own popularity to a duel of monumental proportion. A 3,00 mile horse race across the most formidable terrain in the world - well, except desert California, most of Nevada and nearly all of Arizona - is the primary setting, but the not-too-unhistorically accurate depictions of the Massacre at Wounded Knee and even Buffalo Bill's Wild West Show are the launching pads of this epic tale. Mixing humor, adventure, a fairly intriguing story (one not too complicated by subplots) and terrific location shooting, we are treated to a fairly timeless piece. Audiences in the Thirties would have liked it, and viewers today should appreciate it even more. Turning its back on convention, "Hidalgo" (the name of Hopkins' horse) has a feel of the real thing.  This feeling is garnered as one views real deaths at the hands of swords and guns and spears. These weapons are wielded by warriors, and unlike contemporary offerings are far more believable and realistically rendered. This is not to say the film is gory; but one can feel some of the pain of the men and even animals involved. If films like "Holes" and "Hidalgo" can be made when all eyes and ears are focused on the next big digital computer-enhanced thing then I say bring 'em on.</fullReview>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Mortensen is strong in his role and the action sequences are fun, but this isn't the epic it wants to be.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>HIDALGO (2004)**SPOILERS**THE GOOD:--Though it takes a while to get to them, the action sequences are genuinely enjoyable. The sequence where Hidalgo and some of the sheikh's men rescue Jazira from capture is especially resourceful and entertaining. I enjoyed seeing the variations of weapons and styles used in the action sequences; we get sword fights and all sorts of kitchenware being smashed over people's heads, gunplay and some intense horse racing sequences. And that sandstorm you all saw in the trailer is bloody amazing too.--Predictably, this epic is easy on your eyes and technically well made. There are some great visual effects sequences and the cinematography is Oscar-worthy and the scene where Hopkins prays to the gods to save his horse or whatever (a weird scene that belongs nowhere in this movie) is memorably stunning - I was just gasping all throughout that scene. There are enough stunning long sunset shots to please any "Pearl Harbour" fan too.--Viggo Mortensen is hot property now, and "Hidalgo" only further proves this. He doesn't step a foot wrong here. Mortensen has consistent chemistry with pretty much every cast member, not least Hidalgo himself. Hidalgo is a beautiful horse and has a personality. Mortensen seems to be able to handle these animals like a master, though his brilliant acting skills from Aragorn to Hopkins have me hoping he doesn't give up his day job to professionally train them or something.THE BAD:--What was I watching here? I'm not entirely sure. Screenplay writer John Fusco just never seems to be able to settle on one genre or story. Is this a film focusing on the relationship between a man and his horse? Or the relationship between a princess and a foreign man, which is against the rules? Was this about one man's struggle to overcome doubt and do his best? An epic desert chase film? A film about the courage one town has to combine and overcome its enemies? All of the above? Perhaps?but if one film is going to do all of these things they all need more development and none (save the man/horse relationship) have convinced me very much as far as storytelling is concerned.--Twists in the tale are fun, as long as they are paid off and make sense. Lady Anne Davenport's sudden turn on Hopkins and Hidalgo is rushed and under-developed, we are never given a reason why she would deceive them like this. Her twist is nonsensical and never given a resolution and lacks afterthought, but that said her entire part in the film is annoying and completely unnecessary. Every time the character came on-screen I just kept thinking 'Oh God, this dumb bitch again'.--It may have been the way it happened and all, but this film is exceedingly predictable. I saw the ending a mile away - Hidalgo wins after some strife and competition and everyone - who previously didn't like Hopkins, by the way - cheers after him and looks up to the winner and then Hopkins and Hidalgo split up in a subtle sequence. It's one of those movies where you say to the person next to you 'Oh, I didn't see that coming!' in an overly sarcastic way.RATING:6/10 - Though this is a technically stunning film featuring Mortensen in sizzling form, it's overlong and has an identity crisis. You have to be a patient viewer, but "Hidalgo" is worth checking out if you're a horse lover or cinematography-crazed movie fan.IF YOU LIKED THIS MOVIE I RECOMMEND:The Horse Whisperer (9/10) The Lord of the Rings: The Fellowship of the Ring (9/10) The Lord of the Rings: The Two Towers (10/10) The Lord of the Rings: The Return of the King (10/10) Rabbit-Proof Fence (10/10)</fullReview>

		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Hidalgo provides a splendid evening of entertainment for lovers of the old west and those that respect the Sioux, western and Arabic cultures.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Much has been written about whether the events in the movie Hidalgo are true or even feasible. Whether they are true or not is irrelevant as this is a movie made for entertainment. The cast and crew of Hidalgo have done a tremendous job of creating an entertaining and enjoyable film that is respectful of western, Sioux and Arabic cultures. The characterization of Frank Hopkins in this film is one of the best depictions of cowboy behavior in recent history. Cowboys are respectful, courteous, loyal, hard-working and yet, they do play hard. Inclusion of Lakota Sioux and Arabic dialects with large and easy-to-read English subtitles did not detract from the flow of the film. Some reviewers have commented that Mortensen mumbles too many of his lines. One that is accustomed to listening to Native American dialects might find that the softness, quietness and blending of the sounds is characteristic of this native language. The director did an excellent job in casting this diverse group of actors and each brings distinctive touches to their performance. The cinematographer should be commended for capturing the beauty of each locale, and the scenery is gorgeous. Hidalgo delivers the story of a valiant horse who happened to be owned by a man that stumbled, fell and yet through arduous effort regained his sense of self and place in the world.</fullReview>

		<username>Rob G</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Not too shabby - worth seeing</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a good movie - it avoids a lot of the cliches you'd expect in this type of film. The Arab characters are not stereotyped - the script treats them, their religion and their culture respectfully. In fact, I can't recall a major Hollywood movie that had this much Arabic in it - it's very nice to listen to, and it give the film authenticity. Omar Sharif was terrific - he swings that sword pretty well for being over 70.The film has some minor weaknesses. There are a few cheesy "one-liners" dropped here and there. The cinematography is pretty much Hollywood 101 - it didn't do a good job of providing a good feel for scale of the landscape. I did notice that the sky was faked in a few big action scenes (you can always tell when they do this - the sky looks a little too blue, too perfect; and the light on the actors just doesn't seem to fit). They relied a little too heavy on digital effects - I think a little creative shot taking could have been more effective than seeing two obviously digital cheetahs on the attack.The movie makes up for these shortcomings with actual character development and a good script that's not a sell-out, which are two things many action movies lack these days. While it won't win any Oscars (tm), this film is signifigantly a few steps above an average action flick, which makes it worthy of the $8.00 investment.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-03-03</date>
		<username>Douglas Young (the-movie-guy)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The little horse that could</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Based on a true story in 1890, American cowboy Frank T. Hopkins (Viggo Mortensen) is the first outsider or infidel to be invited by a wealthy Sheik (Omar Sharif) to race in the greatest long-distance horse race ever run. Hopkins was once a dispatch rider for the U.S. cavalry and is known as the greatest long-distance rider in the West. While Hopkins is working in the Buffalo Bill Cody's Wild West Show, the Sheik's emissary challenges him to enter the race, which he accepts. This race has been held every year for the last thousand years, and has only been open to the purest line of Arabian horses ever bred. Hopkins' horse, Hidalgo, a small mixed-breed mustang, was regarded as impure, and therefore shouldn't be in the race with purebred Arabian stallions. The `Ocean of Fire' race is a grueling 3,000-mile survival horse race across the Arabian Desert with the winner receiving $100,000 as prize money and the honor of being the best in the world. The 100-entry race is a battle of endurance and survival and only a few make it to the finish line, alive. If you like western movies, you must see this one. The story line of the little horse and the underdog cowboy racing against the best Arabian horses is a great American classic tale. The movie is long, but the filmmakers keep it interesting from beginning to end by telling stories within stories throughout the race. Some of the funniest scenes are with Hopkins talking to Hidalgo and the horse understanding what he is talking about by giving expressions with his eyes. Hidalgo means Nobleman in Spanish. (Touchstone Pictures, Run Time 2:15, Rated PG-13)(10/10)</fullReview>
	</review>

Hidalgo, a plucky Spanish mustang with beautiful roan and white markings, and Frank Hopkins (Viggo Mortensen), an ex Pony-Express rider, are 2 loyal friends who are invited to partake in a huge tradition across the globe, a race across the formidable sands of the Arabian desert against the most celebrated horses and horsemen of that land. The story and action never stop, and for the lover of good old fashioned swash-buckling adventure, there are plenty of gun and sword battles, a couple of involving subplots, and even some genuine comedy.The cast includes Omar Shariff and Louise Lombard. Even though it's a male-dominated movie and story, the 2 women in the movie (one is a strong-willed Sheik's daughter and the other a ruthless Englishwoman horse fancier) are strongly written and well-acted, not just set filler. Music and costumery are beautiful as well as the cinematography, the setting in the sweltering sands very interesting.I'm glad they still take the time and effort to make movies like this. 		<fullReview>"Hidalgo" tells of the adventures of mustang long rider Frank T. Hopkins who - according to the film - just missed the Indian slaughter at Wounded Knee, appeared in Buffalo Bill's Wild West show, and rode 3000 miles across the Arabian desert in a horse race (circa 1890s). There's a very elegant website which sings the praises of this supposed legend and another not-so-elegant but seemingly more credible website which calls the whole Hopkins tale a hoax. Regardless, the film sticks Mortensen out front as Hopkins along with his trusty paint "Hidalgo" as it takes the audience on a two hour ride through a fictionalized journey from Wounded Knee Creek, SD to the deserts of Arabia in a tinselized, santitized, contrived, and hokey adventure tale with countless hazards and heroics. With lukewarm critical reviews, a somewhat more respectable nod from the public at large, and no awards of consequence, "Hidalgo" should make a good small screen watch for those more interested in a family adventure flick than those interested in history and the real deal. After all, why hitch your pony to a rail when all you have to do is whistle and he'll unhitch himself and come running (duh)? (B-)</fullReview>

The `Ocean of Fire' competition could have been called `a race through hell'. As a matter of fact, Frank Hopkins (in the movie) uses this very description when it is on the verge of shooting Hidalgo, nose bleeding and collapsing in exhaustion, a mustang he calls `brother'.This would have been enough for a great movie. The moviemakers, however, probably very honest and good natured business people, seem to think that they need to do everything they possibly can to give the audience the best value for their money. See what Hidalgo (the movie) ends up having!On the characters department, there are the exotic chief and the liberated princess, both surprisingly westernized, the traditional dark looking villain, the treacherous traitor, the fierce but mutually-respected competitor, the funny bad guy, the funny crazy old man, the alluring but ruthless aristocratic woman, the coal-black heroic warrior, and even a heart-winning slave boy thrown in for good measure.Not enough? How about some spectacle specials. There are killing desert storm, sun darkened by locus (which later becomes breakfast), hideous traps, murderous leopards, blinding eagles, mystic aboriginal chanting, plus kung-fu type fighting, cowboy shooting and an exciting horse race finish. Get the idea?But unfortunately, quantity does not necessarily mean quality.The start of the movie is striking similar with The Last Samurai: a degenerated hero stuck in a cheap road show, resorting to alcoholism to drown out anguishing memories of a massacre. But the similarity ends here, as Hidalgo takes on the line mentioned earlier, a race that is, forgive the comparison, not unlike Cannonball Run in many aspects. There is however a good central theme: will power and determination is more important than pedigree. The theme, unfortunately and ironically, is diluted by the moviemakers' well-intentioned effort to give the audience everything indiscriminately. Amidst some good laughs (the `castrating' scene is a sure-fire) and a distracting kidnap-and-rescue episode (which is far too long), the power and intensity of the race is lost. The ending (the race and the final release of Hidalgo) is uplifting, but falls short of moving the audience to the level of elation that Seabiscuit is able to.The cinematography is good, just as in other similar work that attempt to capture the deadly splendor of the desert, e.g. The English Patient. There is no point, however, in comparing any of these to Lawrence of Arabia. David Lean has done it once and I don't think anybody is able to do it again.Viggo Mortensen has done not too badly. He has brought out the compassionate side of Frank Hopkins quite well, but not enough of the wild spirit. Aragon is a perfect fit that is unlikely to be repeated. Putting that aside, however, Mortensen is heading in the right direction since the somewhat inconsequential roles in A Perfect Murder and Psycho. It's an absolute delight to see Omar Sharif again, particularly with his trade mark crevice between his two front teeth intact! His complete nude (but not full frontal) pose in Mackenna's Gold (1969) was talk of the town at the time. One more actor absolutely must be mentioned: T.J. (according to IMDB's cast listing) who played Hidalgo. This horse can really act. The look he gives when he turns around to see the approaching desert storm should earn him an Oscar.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>"Hidalgo" brings back the nostalgia of the classical swashbuckler film, recalling the era of such stars as Douglas Fairbanks and Errol Flynn. Viggo Mortensen is the protagonist of this action yarn set in the late nineteenth century. I was impressed with Mortensen's understated performance of the cowboy and scout Frank Hopkins, who is haunted by the atrocities done to the Native Americans at Wounded Knee. A man of the earth and a lover of mustang horses, Hopkins takes a bet to compete in a horse race from Syria to Damascus, packing up his horse and traveling to the Middle East! The film is memorable for the beautiful photography of the two continents. The screen writing includes sensitive insights into the various cultures, offering thoughtful character portrayals and treating Islamic and Native American faiths with respect. The fine supporting cast dynamically portrays the colorful characters with Omar Sharif and J.K. Simmons especially memorable in their roles. My only criticism was the excessive and graphic violence in what was intended primarily as a "feel good" movie. The long road to Damascus was often a bloody one. Still, this was a compelling action story filmed in a splendid array of locations and with stunning cinematography.</fullReview>

Now this is not a bad thing to be a clone of an excellent actor like Neeson, it means one is still talented. Due diligence in commentary structuring dictated that research into the real Frank Hopkins be done.Buffalo Bill's Wild West show was one of the most photographed events of that time. In the case of Frank Hopkins, The Long Rider's Guild web site stated in a long list of "Hopkins deceptions", that the truth is that Hopkins was a laborer digging the Philadelphia subway and, "...there is not even a documented photograph of Frank Hopkins in the saddle!" Not news. Okay, so Disney deals with fantasy all the time, so the movie isn't based on true fact as they boasted. Not a strange turn of events for a Hollywood movie to be total horse chips. Fortunately, I am a big fan of cinematography so this mitigated the complete disappointment in lost opportunity for a very good movie. Hopkin's legend derived from an ability to pass fantastic fiction as fact. Disney should have gone to the source on this one. Legions of mediums abound in tinsel town. No excuse not to do a séance. Hopkins agility with the true facts would have assisted the movie plot line. Lacking good consultations the movie, Hidalgo, wanders into a long formula parade of predictable plot lines and clichés. Few surprises sprout.Hasn't Shariff stopped playing the same cliché character movie after movie? From a directorial standpoint this likely is one of the largest failures in the movie. Shariff can be one of the most powerful actors on any screen, but here his characterization fails the litmus test for reality. The potential passion of a racing fanatic gets tripped up when it would have contributed to the plot. The religious aspect of Islam and Bedouin is poorly represented. Despite the recent events these are warm, effervescent people who treat guests intelligently, kindly, lavishly and with forgiveness.Again this is fantasy which serves the plot line little. Had the Bedouin been portrayed correctly as they really are, this would have shifted the plot line to a true believable level. It would have been a better movie.Would I watch the movie again? Yes, knowing that there are worse movies, given little choice I'd watch. The exception to that choice, ... just not on a bus.</fullReview>
