A fine cowboy "yarn" and although strong, rugged, and intelligent, no mustang could beat a purebred Arabian horse in a race of endurance. 		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>"Old fashioned" if you're in the mood but if you're looking for more then "solid and quite unremarkable" is more like it</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Together, Pony Express courier Frank Hopkins and his horse Hidalgo are the greatest cross-country team in the US, perhaps even the world. After becoming bitter and haunted by the slaughter of Native Americans, the pair join a travelling show and it is their billing as "the greatest" that causes offence as far away as the kingdom of Sheikh Riyadh. The Sheikh has a proud tradition of the greatest horses that have proved themselves in a gruelling cross-desert race; Hopkins is told to withdraw the claim or to prove it. Never one to allow his horse to be questioned, Hopkins travels to the Arabia to be the first "infidel" to take part in the race.Hidalgo got some promotion off the back of being Mortensen's first major film coming out of the Lord of the Rings films but even with this it came and went in the cinemas and watching it now I can understand why. I do not mean this to be a criticism but rather just a comment on the fact that the film is, as many have said "old fashioned" or as I would rather say it "solid but yet unremarkable". This is normally the description given to a film that doesn't engage in modern style and substance but rather just delivers a straight "adventure" story set in the past. Hidalgo fits in with this because it does have a solid story and character and provides a narrative that has OK action and dramatic sequences one after another right to the end. Those looking for a sturdy and simple film could do worse.Having said that though, those looking for more may well be bored by Hidalgo because, as well as it does the basics, it doesn't do a huge amount more and while it was interesting I was surprised by how unexciting it was and by how much of a passive observer I had very quickly become. I still thought it was OK but I doubt I will ever find myself recommending to anyone or providing higher praise than "yeah, it was OK" if asked my opinion of it by a friend, colleague or family member. Johnston's direction is fine throughout and he and his cinematographer do have some nice touches in there. Mortensen is suitable for the role but he is able to do more and this film doesn't offer him very much to work with beyond a rugged character. Sharif is a nice bit of casting and works reasonably well while Robinson, Lombard, Taghmaoui and Simmons are all reasonably good in support.Overall then this is a sturdy enough story that makes for a solid film even if it is not particularly special or memorable in any way. The "based on a true story" legend should really have been "based on a tall tale" but it made very little difference to me while watching it. If you are in the mood for an "old fashioned" film then this will deliver that but if you are looking for more than the basics then for you "old fashioned" should translate as "solid, not particularly engaging and quite unremarkable".</fullReview>

		<date>2004-12-30</date>
		<username>Roland E. Zwick (magneteach@aol.com)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>silly but fun adventure flick</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Hidalgo" is one of those rousing, old fashioned adventure yarns that is none too taxing on the brain and offers a veritable feast for the eyes, ears and senses. This is a film for the virtual traveler who prefers to venture to distant times and exotic locales without having to leave the comfort and safety of his own living room armchair. The time is the late Nineteenth Century, the place the Arabian Desert, a brutal, unforgiving and barren wasteland that is the least likely location for a 3,000 mile long horse race in which at least half of the contestants who start out on the journey are destined to die before those lucky enough to survive cross the finish line. In fact, this is less of a "race" and more of an endurance contest, with the men and horses having to cope with hunger, thirst, deadly sandstorms, unbearable heat, and a whole host of other natural impediments on the road to victory.The film is based on the true story of Frank T. Hopkins and his mustang, Hidalgo, who together comprised the greatest long distance horse running team on the American continent. Hopkins was a messenger for the U.S. army who, without realizing the import of what he was doing, delivered the orders to have the Indians brutally massacred at Wounded Knee (Hopkins was himself half Indian and half white). As the film begins, we find him racked with guilt for inadvertently betraying his own people. To assuage his pain, he has since turned to drink and is rotting away as one of the sideshow attractions in Buffalo Bill's Wild West Show, alongside such "celebrities" as Sitting Bull and Calamity Jane. When word of Hidalgo's prowess reaches Arabia, the horse and the rider are invited to participate in the grueling desert race, even though Hopkins would be the first "infidel" to do so, a fact that causes a great deal of consternation among the local Islamic riders. To say that the film is rabid in its pro-American sentiments is putting it mildly, for despite all the obstacles and the threat of imminent death, Hopkins agrees to take up the challenge not only as a way of bringing some purpose and meaning back into his own life, but as a method of teaching those upstart Mid Eastern types a thing or two about what real Americans - both human and equine - are made of."Hidalgo" is at its best when it focuses on the eye-popping vistas of the sand-swept desert or the intricate, survival-of-the-fittest details of the race itself. The story, on the other hand, is pure melodramatic hokum and the numerous secondary characters are silly and sketchily developed. Even Hopkins is little more than a cardboard cutout - and Viggo Mortensen does little in the way of acting to bring any noticeable depth to the character. But who really cares? This is, after all, nothing more than an unadulterated Bedouin fantasy for Arabian Nights enthusiasts. Heck, this is the kind of movie in which the horse unties his own reins when he and his human companions have to hightail it out of Dodge (or the Arabian equivalent thereof) to escape the clutches of an evil horse thief, kidnapper and brigand. Actually, the most solid performance by far is turned in by none other than Omar Sharif (as the "Sheik of Sheiks"), who returns to the desert setting that made him a household name in "Lawrence of Arabia." "Hidalgo" is no "Lawrence," of course, but the film's visual scope, high-flying adventure and intense action scenes make it a first rate popcorn entertainment, bringing us back to the type of movie going experience we thought had indeed been lost to the sands of time.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-10-31</date>
		<username>Sisiutil</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Corny fun</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Hidalgo isn't exactly the most original movie to come along in awhile, but it certainly is a lot of fun.The movie is based on a real event in the life of a real person, Pony Express rider and horse racer Frank Hopkins. Hopkins and his horse Hidalgo--both supposedly past their prime--enter a gruelling race across the Arabian desert.We have elements of the Western, of sports movies, and of travelogues here. The film-makers seemed determined to insert all the expected elements--dare I say clichés--of all those genres into the film. But it's all done so well that it's not only forgivable, its comforting. You expect to see things like the underdog, washed-up, conflicted hero coming to terms with himself, the come-from-behind win, treacherous opponents, beautiful but harsh desert scenery, etc., etc.There's even some meaningful sub-text thrown in. Hopkins is conflicted over his mixed parentage (his mother was a Native American); this ties into the fact that his horse is a Mustang, an "impure" breed, while all his opponents are purebred Arabians.In addition, the fact that the film is set in the Middle East with the majority of characters being Arab Muslims is, of course, significant. The hero finds himself in conflict with some while winning the respect and friendship of others. Perhaps there's some American wishful thinking going on here, but the fact that ANY Arab Muslim is portrayed in a positive light in a Hollywood film is something worthy of praise. And they don't smack you over the head with it, which is good, because this isn't a "message" movie, it's entertainment.My biggest complaint about the film is the interlude where it delves into Indiana Jones-style adventure involving Hopkins (and his horse) rescuing an Arab princess. This is where the film goes completely Hollywood for several minutes, and it has nothing to do with the race. It's evidence that the film-makers--or more likely the suits financing it--didn't trust their material enough, and that's too bad, if all-too typical.Nonetheless, even that misplaced sequence is entertaining to watch, as is the whole film. Mortensen doesn't really stretch himself here--aside from the hair and the accent, Hopkins is a lot like Aragorn--but he's does a typically excellent job with the role and delivers his many down-home, plain-spoken one-liners with cowboy aplomb. It's also a film you can watch with the whole family, and that's something these days.8/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Parallels with contempory Middle Eastern Events</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What are the hidden messages in this film? At first it gives an impression of just being another "Raiders of the Lost Ark" clone but perhaps there is a deeper message within. The Horse Hidalgo is a mustang and as such represents modern America: a cocktail of different races.The rider is the new America, brash, swaggering with a past.The good Arab is represented by Omar Sharif: the bad Arab his nephew represents Saddam Hussein/Osman Bin Laden. Sharif has a fascination with all things Western and embraces elements of western culture. His daughter has a deep desire to be emancipated and embrace western ideals but the old Arab culture is holding her back. In the background we have the duplicititous Englishwoman who represents the "Old Europe" who is trying to frustrate Americas ambitions ( the Horse race).In the end good prevails over evil.New America has come out on tops.Some hearts and minds have been won over. The gift of a colt revolver to Omar Sharif is symbolic of American Military Aid? Anyway apart from that it was a very enjoyable film.</fullReview>
	</review>

If you are an animal lover, you will be in tears during some scenes and cheering and shouting during others... There is beauty involved when each of these morals is brought to the surface, but never in a 'smack-you-upside-the-head' sort of way....Watch the movie for its beautiful animals and its beautiful juxtaposition of thoughts against actions.9 out of 10 stars!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Pretty good yarn</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Based on Frank Hopkin's own published accounts of his life, this film has a decent cast featuring some pretty good acting. There is adventure, a little romance (but not much), some gorgeous horses and a race that, by many other accounts, never occurred. To hear some tell it, it seems that Mr. Hopkins may have built himself up to be more of an adventurer than he actually was. But, insofar as this film is concerned, the truth is neither here nor there. The film is well made, with, as I said, a good cast. Viggo Mortensen stars as Frank Hopkins. Omar Sharif is the Arab sheik who puts on a 3,000 mile race across the desert each year. Frank is given the opportunity to compete in this race with his American Mustang named Hidalgo. It is a race of pureblooded Arabian horses against this American horse, and, of course, the believers against the infidel American. Everybody cheats, except, of course, Mr. Hopkins. There is treachery, a few raids by bandits, and otherwise, enough action to satisfy anyone. Overall, a pretty decent film. I gave it an 8.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Enjoyable show - Spoilers</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After reading the comments I almost didn't go. But I enjoyed the show.Some of the comments here about Muslims and how Arabs are portrayed didn't make sense to me, since the movie was set in the late 1800's. About the only thing I could see which would be a comment on our times was that Cowboy made it clear that Americans knew how to get information by torturing someone. There were some remarks here about horse riding - sure enough, I didn't think that Cowboy looked like he was one with his horse. But riding is indeed something that takes effort - although I have had limited (bad) experience. I was on a horse one time that broke into a gallop when we entered an open field (after it had tried to scrape me off on some trees), following friends who were experienced riders. It was unbelievable, one of the most beautiful experiences I have ever had, as the horse shifted into a smooth wavelike motion. Everyone should experience it at least once in their lives. I think that gallop across the field might have been the only time a horse hasn't tried to just get rid of me, because they could tell I didn't know what I was doing. There was very little portrayed of religion, a lot of the standard fatalism that you hear ("it is in Allah's hands") but most of the characters, good or bad, worked hard to try to get outcomes that they wanted, they didn't just sit around. (Reminds me of a book of proverbs a professor I knew used to quote occasionally - the one I remember was "Trust in Allah, but tie up your camel.") As far as the veil removal - Cowboy did rescue her after all, and apparently even in the late 1800's some tribes did allow their women to remove their veils. Perhaps more evil was the English woman who mocked the tradition by wearing a see through veil herself in at least one scene, presumably Western women did (or should I say do) this to create a sense of drama, mystery and allure - but that was okay because she was a bad person overall anyway. The drinking, of Cowboy, the English woman, was tolerated by the Muslims, but again it was not presented in an attractive way - it was clearly presented as a weakness of Cowboy and English woman was a bad person, so who cared if she drank. A few Mustangs in a park are fine by me, but where I live they have wild horses that compete for grazing with native Elk, so I am happy to see that there are occasional culls. The horses are supposed to end up getting adopted but I suspect that many of them end up in dog food. It had some of the standard Hollywood formulas - the sportsmanship of the Prince who at the end swallowed his disappointment and gave his judgment about what a fantastic horse Hildago was (rather than sulking or continuing the insults he had hurled along the way). But an effort was made to convey the close bond between Bedouin and their horses - the guy who accepted that he was going to die in the hot mud sinkhole was begging his horse not to come near. Some people walked out of the theater when I saw the show, but I found it enjoyable and it seemed most of the people in the audience felt the same.</fullReview>

Mustangs are not a pure breed, such as Arabians are. They are more accustomed to foraging for food and seeking shelter from the elements. I have always had a thing for Viggo, and seeing him in a cowboy hat just did it for me. It seemed that he had a natural rapport with horses, and the horse who played Hidalgo and Viggo had perfect chemistry. He referred to the horse as "brother." What does that symbolize? The relationship that was portrayed by the two of them in the movie made me long to run home after the movie and see my horse. Never in my life has a movie made me hope, then cry, then hope and cry all over again. In the end, hope won out, though my husband's sleeves were wet with my tears, as well as his own. Everybody should see this movie. You will come away with a new respect for the relationship between man and animal and a new meaning of the word "hope." Viggo, aka "Frank" never let his brother give up, and his brother never let him. 		<username>garyvanhorn</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>A sure fire cure for insomnia.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>You would think that a half-Indian cowboy riding a wild mustang in the world's most grueling endurance race across the Arabian desert, braving raiders, cheaters, sandstorms, and the blistering desert environment would be quite exciting, you would be wrong though. Hidalgo wants to be an old fashioned adventure tale but what it actually ends up being is an often clichéd snoozefest.Hidalgo is supposedly based on the real life exploits of Frank T. Hopkins (Viggo Mortenson). His name is right, everything else is almost pure fiction....and it shows. I wanted to like the movie, I really did, but the race was rather boring, the characters were walking clichés and stereotypes, and the action felt contrived and out of place amid the endless ride through the desert. The scene that sealed the fate of the movie shows Hidalgo (the horse) collapsing in the desert, blood running from his nostrils. His injuries resulted from a fall into a spiked pit trap laid by bandits paid by a rival to ensure Hidalgo does not win the race. Hopkins cuts out the spear that clearly lamed Hidalgo and cauterizes the wound then they bravely carry on to the final stage of the race. Anyway, after Hidalgo collapses, Hopkins does a ghost dance to summon his ancestors for aid. Mysterious, ghostly figures appear, seeming to offer their support to the beleaguered duo of horse and man. Hidalgo rises up and then races the two other top competitors to the finish line.I won't tell you who wins, but given the hopelessly clichéd nature of the movie you can probably guess all on your own how it ends. Movies like this are best when the journey is more important than the destination. When the journey is less than interesting, the destination becomes completely pointless, much like this movie.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-11-08</date>
		<username>Andy (film-critic)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Mister... you can say anything you want about me. I'm gonna have to ask you not to talk about my horse that way.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Hidalgo could quite possibly be retitled to the worst pre-Summer release of 2004. I am not sure what I was expecting when I first chose to pick Hidalgo up and actually watch it. Perhaps it was the visions on the box of an evil Sheik, a dust storm, or a wild-eye cowboy looking for adventure that put me in the mood. If I would have only known that the cover of the box ultimately shows the best parts of the film and the rest of this two hour production is nothing more than clichéd lines and filler. This was a horrible film from the beginning to end. Viggo Mortensen, so powerful in his role of Aragorn in Lord of the Rings, follows-up as a cheesy, clichéd cowboy that mutters 'Yes sir' and 'No ma'am' throughout the film. His character has no real depth or value, thus causing us to question his reasons for being the 'good guy'. Let me explain. We begin out film with Mortensen running a race then proceeding to punch someone in the face. Then he becomes an alcoholic exhibitionist, then he retires off to the big sand race where he continues to find trouble. Where, in the middle of all of this, do we see the compassion behind his eyes, the warmth around his smile? I must have missed it between the several minutes of shuteye I was getting while attempting to forge through this film. I must say that I did relate to one of the scenes. Closer to the end Mortensen feels like all is lost and no hope can be seen on the horizon, he is burnt by the sun and tired. Hidalgo even wants to end this debauchery. I really connected with this scene. While I was watching this movie, I felt as if I was burnt and could not continue forward. I wanted to give up, but after seeing the time on the DVD over two hours, I knew that home was somewhere in sight. So, like Frank Hopkins, I fought forward and survived.Outside of Mortensen's butchering of Frank Hopkins, we also have veteran actor Omar Sharif prejudicing the role of Jazira, a very wealthy Sheik. By the time we were introduced to this character, I already had a bad taste in my mouth so I was bitter toward Sharif, but he honestly brought nothing new to the table. I found his passion of the Wild West awkward and silly. All he did was add more wood to the already burning fire known as Hidalgo. I needed somebody in this film to speak up and stand out as the prizewinner. I was looking for that one actor that was obviously doing this film because he believed in the role, not the Disney money. I never once found passion in any of our characters eyes (even the horse). This caused me to question the sincerity of this film. Were our people making this film just to get another film out there, or were they excited about the story. I would have to go with the first option instead of the second for this film.As critics, we cannot just blame the actors because they are merely following the direction of the man behind the camera, so I would like to focus our next bit of attention on Joe Johnston. He made three grave errors on his part when releasing this film. The first is the fact that he quoted us, the audience, that this was an epic. This was no epic. There was nothing epical about it. It was a horse racing film. To call it anything else would just be giving it a better jacket. The bread and butter was that it was a horse racing film, nothing more nothing less. With this in mind, why did the horse race start 45-50 minutes into the film? I came into this DVD hoping to see some racing, not a dimwitted character stumbling through his lines. I needed more, but was handed less. I felt like Oliver Twist. Johnston's second error was another debunked fact when he said that this film was based on a true story. Nobody has ever proved that Hopkins ran all these races. He said he did to gain notoriety, but it has never been proved. This should have been stated at the end of the film. It would have only turned the heat up on this burning critic who wasted several hours of his life with this film. Finally, the last mistake that Johnston did with this film deals with the cinematography. It was a jumbled mess to say the least. There was no consistency behind the camera. I felt as if Johnston took images from The Mummy and from an Oliver Stone film and called them his own. I wanted Johnston to build his own name and style with this movie, but I could not find one original scene. His camera work was shoddy and uninventive. Closer to the end, it just became incoherent. Johnston was trying so many different styles that we, the audience, suddenly became lost also in this desert ? a place I didn't want to be on a Sunday night.Then there was Hidalgo. I could tell from the first time that I saw this steed turn around and look at Mortensen that he was not happy with this film. I kept waiting to hear him talk or at least do a Mr. Ed impersonation, but it never occurred. Every time I looked at Hidalgo, I could only see glue. The glue that was missing from this film to keep it held together. Near the final scene when Hidalgo is giving it his final push, I leaned over to my wife and asked her if Mortensen finally put this Mustang in fifth gear. HA! We needed some form of humor to compensate for this poor film.The only redeeming value (and it is small) about this film that I thought was interesting was the connection between the Muslims and the Native Americans. Whether this is accurate or not, I felt it was helpful in seeing the connection between Sharif and Mortensen. They fought and disliked each other, but they were of a similar culture, and I enjoyed watching that short minute before getting right back into the non-existent race. May I make a final suggestion? If you want to see a real horse race, go to the tracks. It would be a better adventure than you witnessed with this film and you might, just might, win back your money that you wasted on this filth.Grade: * out of *****</fullReview>
	</review>

	<review>
		<date>2005-08-10</date>
		<username>LilyDaleLady</username>
		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>Very disappointing, historical nonsense</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>An Oscar of some kind should definitely be won by...the people who made the TRAILER for "Hidalgo". Because they certainly made a sow's ear look like a silk purse. The trailer was exciting enough that my friend's children wanted to see it badly, and so did I -- I am sucker for any kind of horse movie -- so I took them to see it.What a disappointment! For starters, apparently everyone is well aware that this is NOT based on a "true story", as nothing in Mr. Hopkin's self-aggrandizing fairy tales bears any scrutiny. However, plenty of wonderful stories are entirely based on fiction -- my only beef is saying that "Hidalgo" is a true story, when it clearly is NOT.Even as a fantasy based on the myth of a "superhorse" -- a tough little Mustang stallion who is 1/4 painted pony, and can outrace seemingly larger and tougher purebreds -- "Hilalgo" is a failure. Not nearly enough time is spent on exploring the relationship between cowboy and horse. Certainly the film is influenced heavily by "Seabiscuit", with the concept of the unremarkable looking horse who has so much heart and spirit that he can defeat all the thoroughbreds. This is a valid story -- Seabiscuit was certainly a real horse -- but nobody bothered to take this a step further or develop an intimate connection between the viewer and the equine performer (or performers, as I imagine there was more than one Hiladgo cast). So Hidago himself ends up just a "trick pony", who accomplishes miraculous things for no particular reason except that it suits the desired ending of the story.Viggo Mortensen is a particular disappointment here, following on his glorious role in LOTR, and I imagine a lot of fans (including myself) were let down by this wooden, tired performance. I've seen Viggo in enough other films to know he CAN act, but you'd never guess it here. Also this part seems written for an actor more like Kevin Costner -- the aw-shucks type -- and not Viggo's sort of reserved and quiet demeanor. In a supporting role, Omar Shariff is a pleasure to see in one of his last role. Newcomer Zuliekha Robinson seems to be the love interest, but the filmmakers draw back from developing anything, preferring to keep Hopkins character a "loner" (which is odd, since the real life Frank Hopkins was married twice, and not to a Arab princess). This is odd prudery, even for a kid's film.My biggest beef against "Hilalgo" is the manner in which the horses, and especially the title character, are treated. For starters, Viggo is a very large man, and Hidalgo a rather small horse -- scenes where Viggo is riding Hilalgo are painfully awkward. At the minimum, a man Viggo's size would be very uncomfortable on such a small animal. It seems to me that the horse playing Hidalgo was chosen largely for his exceptionally beautiful markings -- he is indeed gorgeous -- and not for any characteristics that would make him a good long distance racer.While I think highly of mustangs and paint ponies and all Western breeds, it is grossly unfair and unrealistic to suggest that a horse from such a background could outrun a desert-bred Arabian. This isn't just a matter of pedigree, it's a matter of CLIMATE. The Arabian desert is far hotter and drier than the American desert, plus it's considerably larger. The "Ocean of Fire" race is depicted as killing off many of the desert-bred native horses, yet newcomer Hidalgo adapts immediately to the harshness and outruns them. I should say that I find it hard to accept that horse-loving Arabic tribesman would subject their beloved horses to anything so brutal that clearly results in the death of the majority of animals (and many of the riders).Towards the end of the race, Hidalgo is badly injured with a spear -- an injury so extreme that Frank is considering putting the horse out of his misery. On top of that, Hidalgo has pretty much collapsed from heat exhaustion and thirst. However, in the film, the horse is shown to get up after these atrocious (and unnecessary) injuries and GALLOP AT FULL SPEED to the finish, beating numerous healthy, uninjured and well-rested Arabian stallions. This is not only appalling stupid, but cruel. It suggests that it's pretty much OK to take a horse into an alien environment, subject the poor animal to horrific conditions such as extreme thirst, attack from wild animals, spears, heat stroke and so forth and then have him race happily to the finish line...and for what? to win a bunch of money. (I certainly can't see anything in the HORSE'S interest there.)Basically, this kind of treatment results in DEAD HORSES. If you love horses, this is simply appalling, horrifying and unacceptable at any level. "Hidalgo" feels like a story which is saying "It's OK to abuse a horse nearly to death, as long as you win a lot of money". It should also be noted that the real life Mr. Hopkins did not buy up thousands of acres of Western land and establish a mustang sanctuary, as described in the film. Even today, there is controversy about the wild mustang herds in the Southwest and whether they should be hunted down and processed into pet food.Lame on absolutely every level, "Hidalgo" does not work as an adventure story, fantasy, cowboy story, historical truth, or even just a horse story. As a horse lover, I was doubled up in pain during nearly the whole film. If this actually cost $100 million to film, someone needs to be pursued for fraud. There are numerous quite good horse films out there -- "Seabiscuit", "Black Stallion", "Phar Lap" -- please do yourself a favor and rent one of those, and leave "Hidalgo" where it belongs...at the bottom of the discount bin....</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>In 1890 it appears there was little enough for a cowboy to do, so why not win some money racing the titular mustang, Hidalgo, against 100 of the best Arabian horses in the world-in Arabia! Viggo Mortensen, playing the legendary cavalry-courier Frank Hopkins, who supposedly won about 400 long-distance races, negotiates the sands, wily Sheikh Riyadh (Omar Sharif), his doe-eyed daughter, Jazira (Zuleikha Robinson), and a nefarious Brit blonde Lady Ann Davenport (Louise Lombard) to-Well, I'm not going to spoil the outcome because that is the only plot point worth anything.This is `Seabiscuit' on sand, a Western in the East with all the Hollywood clichés perfectly preserved from sun and wind. Does Davenport lust after the studly cowboy? Does the young daughter hope to snag the cowboy and toss off her veil? Is there a wicked Arabian contestant, guided by the scheming Brit, who will stop at nothing to win? These are just a few of the relentless plot antiquities chosen by director Joe Johnston (`Jurassic Park III') to move a weary plot to its obvious conclusion.I can't figure out how the film couldn't be suspenseful, but I began to feel it was all in real time as I reached for my bottled water in sympathy for Hidalgo and the cowboy. There was, however, a hint of intelligence when the idea of `infidel' was mentioned. Sheikh Riyadh refusing to touch the cowboy infidel lest sheikh lose his ability to see the future was intriguing, as was Riyadh's unwillingness even to touch the infidel. The Muslim culture is the most interesting part of the film. Contemporary mid-Eastern attitudes toward the West translate appropriately at the turn of the 20th century.`Hidalgo' might have been released last year. Now you know why it was not. It's arid and slow. Lightened only briefly by a simple shot of Sharif playing cards in a tent, a sly reference to his mastery of Bridge, something eminently better for him to do than playing a fatuous sheikh. Jizara asks Hopkins, "Why do I feel that you truly see me when others do not?" The answer is, like the film, obvious: She's taken off her veil. Robert Lynd said, `In choosing the winner of a horse race, a good guess may beat all the skill and all the special knowledge in the world.' I'm guessing Hopkins wins and the movie loses.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Frank Hopkins has the qualities of a truly legendary figure. I know Hollywood likes to embellish the story to make it more exciting. Hopkins rode mustangs because they had the strength to endure hardship and keep going. It would have been better if the producers had noted the movie were based on a true legend at the start instead of the end credits. 		<shortDescription>Not as much an epic as the ads want to make us believe.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It says that the movie "Hidalgo" is based on a true story. It probably should have said, 'based on a real person'. The story is mostly about a 3000-mile endurance race in 1890 across Arabia that Frank Hopkins and his Mustang 'Hidalgo' competed in. However, historians cannot find any evidence that such a race ever was held, much less held 'for centuries' as claimed in the script. Putting that aside, it has much of the flavor of an old time western - horses, guns, and people dying in the desert, all taking place in the Arabian desert. It also has the mandatory beautiful women, one a crooked British lady, the other a repressed Arab. Although a bit long at just over 2 hours, it is mostly entertaining, as long as you remember that it is a fable, not a biography.SPOILERS follow, don't read any further. Arabs came to America to protest the claim that Hidalgo was the best endurance horse in the world. They claimed their champion Arabian horse was the best, all that resulted in Hopkins and Hidalgo sailing across the ocean to compete. The competition is made more difficult by scheming to prevent Hidalgo from winning, but Hopkins eventually gets the upper hand. The Arabian princess also wants to help Hidalgo win, as it is the only way she will be spared marrying someone she cares nothing about. The race is hard, many die, and literally minutes before Hopkins is about to shoot the worn out Hidalgo to put him out of his misery, they see two other horses coming, Hidalgo gets up, and without saddle race to the finish line and win. Hopkins uses the prize to go back to Oklahoma and purchase from the government all the hundreds of penned up wild Mustangs and turn them loose again, for the Native Americans.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-04-05</date>
		<username>tessies</username>
		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>Lazy or sloppy research (at best)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Hildago is a very poorly done horse movie. The research is some of the worst I've ever seen for the genre, and many children could have done better. The race on which the film focuses never existed, and the screenwriter has missed an opportunity to educate the public about a people of which it knows little. Sure, there are Hollywood stereotypes of Arabs aplenty (many examples are to be found in Hidalgo). However, most people have heard nothing of bedouin culture, and in this film John Fusco makes a mockery of it. These are the horse-breeding tribes of the desert. The people who greatly revere their horses. Yet here they are supposedly participating in a yearly race for over a thousand years in which their beloved horses routinely perish. The idea is pretty insulting as well as inaccurate, but the bedouin are not a powerful group so attacking their culture is apparently acceptable. The other really glaring error (among many) was the depiction of the treatment of guests. In this movie, there were many insults heaped on Hopkins by the bedouin. However, one of the oldest and most widely practised customs of the bedouin was the great hospitality shown to guests. Even someone who arrived at an enemy's tent and asked to be a guest was treated with honor and given the best the tribe had to offer. But Hopkins, who is an *invited* guest (by a sheikh no less), is on the receiving end of multiple nasty comments, and it is done in such a way that it seems routine to those present. Whereas if remarks in the movie were really said they would have brought great shame on the whole tribe. But that's Hollywood for you--make fun of the most important customs of a particular group, as long as that particular group doesn't have a way to defend itself.As to the horse scenes, well, they were beyond unbelievable. In one scene Hidalgo is apparently exhausted from lack of water, exertion, and the heat and Hopkins is shown dirty, sweaty, and sunburned (even to having chapped lips). But Hidalgo looks fresh as a daisy. His white markings are pristine. There is no sweat or dirt at all on him. He looks like a very healthy (and cool horse) that is lying down. Unfortunately, the movie has too many of these credulity-straining images. Give this film a pass and try a good horse movie like Seabiscuit, Phar Lap, or My Friend Flicka.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Way Off Course</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have nothing against fictionalizing 'history' (more about that below)-- after all, this is a movie. First and foremost it has to have cinematic entertainment value -- it should be a fun visual story.The problem is, it seeks to do this only by robbing other movie predecessors. They ride by in procession: "Lawrence of Arabia", "Last Samurai", "Thunderheart", "Raiders...", the sandstorm of "The Mummy"/"English Patient" ilk. Primarily this steals from "Gladiator": evidenced in the film speed/filter/editing rhythm changes during the 'Wounded Knee' prologue, the warrior picking up and feeling the soil of the battlefield, the camera movement during the sword/spear fights...It tries to carry too much at once, so it ends up tripping over the baggage left and right...there's no flow here, no élan.As for the history, this was a huge missed opportunity. Frank Hopkins was, by what few accounts there are of him, a notorious yarn-spinner, and he was full of canny know-how. How they could have made something of that! Something in the direction of "Sweet and Lowdown", or even "Interview with the Vampire"; some kind of story within the story, some ambivalence over the narrator's believability. Alas, no...we just get a dead story with a lifeless actor...would we know Mortensen was even alive if he weren't threatened with dismemberment? Even "Dances with Wolves" had more life because of the wonderful canvas it was painted on...this has zip, nada, no value and goes nowhere but in circles.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Omar Sharif is believable in the role of the sheik, even though he doesn't have so many lines in this movie. I think it's an easy-to-watch Oriental movie, because it doesn't give you the thrill you might expect when going to the movies. An Oriental movie is usually an exotical one. "Hidalgo" proves to have too many Western elements. Oriental people are seen as narrow-minded villains and, of course, a Western man should always be the winner. The Ocean of Fire could have been a wonderful experience. Instead, the director focuses more on human emotions, even though they are not so well developed. John has memories from the past while being at Wounded Knee, he is attracted by Jazira who proves to be a very beautiful woman, he is deeply affected by Hidalgo's injuries etc. There are too many elements and no one is developed in a proper manner. I would have personally preferred a development of Jazira's and John's emotional relationship, maybe just one passionately kiss, words of wisdom, an exteriorization of their emotions, probably a wedding at the end of the movie. The possibilities are unlimited.Omar Sharif is a very talented actor, but the script doesn't rise to his value, because the character he plays seems to be an easy-to-be-influenced kind of man, who doesn't stand up for a real sheik. A real sheik would have probably sentenced John to death with no discussions, even if his assumptions weren't real, as he is the one in power in those lands.Zuleikha Robinson is a very beautiful woman and has the potential of a successful young actress. I would like to see her in more movies, particularly in epoque kind of movies, as they are my favorite, because the past is more interesting than the present. One who doesn't know the mistakes of the past is condemned to repeat them."Jumanji" is probably Joe Johnston's most attractive movie, because it has the flavor of a real thriller. I enjoyed it from the beginning to the end. Kirsten Dunst is a beautiful talented young actress and made her character believable, but she did her best in Neil Jordan's "Interview with the Vampire: The Vampire Chronicles" while playing a hot young vampire, Brad Pitt's partner. Kirsten said once she was taught how to kiss by Brad Pitt. I would have enjoyed to be in his shoes."Jurassic Park 3" is probably Joe's worst movie, because it seems to be a too childish movie, with no believable climaxes. It's a man hunt, no interesting dialogue, not believable plot kind of movie. Compared to "Hidalgo", it's by far the most commercial one. It should have been one single movie "Jurassic Park", Steven Spielberg's masterpiece, and no sequels, because a sequel will never have the flavor of the original."Hidalgo" is a new experience for Viggo Mortensen. He played different interesting characters in his career. In movies like "The Lord of the Rings" or "A Perfect Murder" he made believable too strong characters. Aragorn is a powerful, emotional, friendly, brave character and David Shaw is a frightening, hot man, who seduces Emily Bradford Taylor. Gwyneth Paltrow and Liv Tyler are too wonderful on-set partners. Liv Tyler probably has deep emotional memories from the sets, because no one can forget Viggo. A powerful warrior, Aragorn and an out-of-this-world beautiful princess, Arwen would make a perfect couple. Chris Martin is probably jealous, because Gwyneth played alongside so many hot young actors, Viggo especially.I give this movie 6/10, because it didn't give me the thrill I was looking for, except for one scene where Hidalgo is attacked by two cheetahs, created using the attractive CGI technology and another one, when John tries to save Jazira, who was kidnapped by Prince Bin Al Reeh, the Oriental type of villain. I hope Joe Johnston will make a critically acclaimed movie and leave these easy-to-digest movies.Have a nice day!</fullReview>
